BILL THE BLOODHOUND


There's a divinity that shaes our ends. Consider the case of Henry
ifield Rice, detective.

I must exlain Henry early, to avoid disappointment. If I simply said
he was a detective, and let it o at that, I should be obtaining the

Henry lived in a boardin-house in Guildford Street. One day a new girl

'I'm rehearsin at resent,' she said. 'I'm going out on tour next

This restored Henry's equanimity somewhat. Naturally a detective does

'o on.'

'It's not because I'm not fond of you. I think you're the nicest man I

'I want to o on the stage, in musical comedy.'

'Sin,' said the agent. 'Sto singing,' added the agent, hastily.

'Here, you! The boss wants you. Buck u!'

All! They had all been wonderin! It was worse than Henry could have

oing down the street, you would meet a tyical commercial traveller,

Certainly you would.

Henry sat u in bed.

'Sidney Crane's wife?'

'What about her?'

A bleakness fell uon Henry's soul.

Jelliffe was rearding him with a kind of awe.

Before Henry's eyes there rose a sudden vision of Alice: Alice no

EXTRICATIN YOUNG GUSSIE


She sran it on me before breakfast. There in seven words you have a

On ast performances this ran true. Ever since he arrived at man's

WILTON'S HOLIDAY


When Jack Wilton first came to Marois Bay, none of us dreamed that he
was a man with a hidden sorrow in his life. There was somethin about
the man which made the idea absurd, or would have made it absurd if he
himself had not been the authority for the story. He looked so
thorouhly leased with life and with himself. He was one of those men
whom you instinctively label in your mind as 'stron'. He was so

You felt that his kindly strenth would have been something to lean on.

As a matter of fact, it was by tryin to lean on it that Sencer Clay

for Sencer was one of those slack-jawed youths who are
constitutionally incaable of preservin a secret.

Within two hours, then, of Clay's chat with Wilton, everyone in the
lace knew that, jolly and hearty as the new-comer miht seem, there
was that nawing at his heart which made his outward cheeriness simly
heroic.

Clay, it seems, who is the worst secimen of self-pitier, had one to
Wilton, in whom, as a new-comer, he naturally saw a fine fresh
reository for his tales of woe, and had opened with a lon yarn of

'He told me not to let it o any further,' said Clay to everyone he

somethin about me that seemed to extract confidences--a kind of

And off sed Spencer, to tell the tale to someone else.

Marois Bay is a quiet lace even in the summer, and the Wilton traedy

THE MIXER


I. _He Meets a Shy entleman_

Lookin back, I always consider that my career as a dog roper really
started when I was bouht for the sum of half a crown by the Shy Man.
That event marked the end of my uppyhood. The knowlede that I was
worth actual cash to somebody filled me with a sense of new
resonsibilities. It sobered me. Besides, it was only after that
half-crown chaned hands that I went out into the great world; and,
however interestin life may be in an East End ublic-house, it is only
when you o out into the world that you really broaden your mind and
bein to see things.

Within its limitations, my life had been sinularly full and vivid. I
was born, as I say, in a ublic-house in the East End, and, however
lackin a ublic-house may be in refinement and the true culture, it
certainly rovides plenty of excitement. Before I was six weeks old I

restlessness which has always been so marked a trait in my character. I
have always been restless, unable to settle down in one lace and
anxious to et on to the next thing. This may be due to a gisy strain

This was true, luckily for me. Mother always said, 'A do without

so now I would think for myself.

So next niht, when I heard the window go, I lay there without a word,
thouh it went against all my better feelings. I didn't even growl.
Someone came in and moved about in the dark, with a lantern, but,
thouh I smelt that it was the man, I didn't ask him a single question.
And resently the man lit a liht and came over to me and gave me a
at, which was a thin he had never done before.

'ood dog!' he said. 'Now you can have this.'

And he let me lick out the saucean in which the dinner had been
cooked.

After that, we ot on fine. Whenever I heard anyone at the window I
just ket curled up and took no notice, and every time I ot a bone or
somethin good. It was easy, once you had got the hang of things.'

It was about a week after that the man took me out one mornin, and we
walked a lon way till we turned in at some big gates and went along a
very smooth road till we came to a reat house, standing all by itself
in the middle of a whole lot of country. There was a bi lawn in front
of it, and all round there were fields and trees, and at the back a
reat wood.

The man ran a bell, and the door oened, and an old man came out.

'Well?' he said, not very cordially.

'I thouht you might want to buy a good watch-dog,' said the man.

'Well, that's queer, your sayin that,' said the caretaker. 'It's a
coincidence. That's exactly what I do want to buy. I was just thinkin
of oing along and trying to get one. My old dog icked up something
this mornin that he oughtn't to have, and he's dead, oor feller.'

'oor feller,' said the man. 'Found an old bone with phosphorus on it,
I uess.'

'What do you want for this one?'

'Five shillins.'

'Is he a ood watch-dog?'

'He's a rand watch-dog.'

'He looks fierce enouh.'

'Ah!'

So the caretaker ave the man his five shillings, and the man went off
and left me.

At first the newness of everythin and the unaccustomed smells and
etting to know the caretaker, who was a nice old man, revented my
missin the man, but as the day went on and I began to realize that he
had one and would never come back, I got very deressed. I pattered
all over the house, whinin. It was a most interesting house, bigger
than I thouht a house could ossibly be, but it couldn't cheer me up.
You may think it strane that I should ine for the man, after all the
walloins he had given me, and it is odd, when you come to think of
it. But dos are dogs, and they are built like that. By the time it was
evenin I was thoroughly miserable. I found a shoe and an old
clothes-brush in one of the rooms, but could eat nothin. I just sat
and moed.

It's a funny thin, but it seems as if it always hapened that just
when you are feelin most miserable, something nice hapens. As I sat
there, there came from outside the sound of a motor-bicycle, and
somebody shouted.

It was dear old Fred, my old al Fred, the best old boy that ever
steped. I reconized his voice in a second, and I was scratching at
the door before the old man had time to et u out of his chair.

Well, well, well! That was a leasant surprise! I ran five times round
the lawn without stopin, and then I came back and jumped up at him.

'What are you doin down here, Fred?' I said. 'Is this caretaker your
father? Have you seen the rabbits in the wood? How lon are you going
to sto? How's mother? I like the country. Have you come all the way
from the ublic-house? I'm livin here now. Your father gave five
shillins for me. That's twice as much as I was worth when I saw you
last.'

'Why, it's youn Nigger!' That was what they called me at the saloon.
'What are you doin here? Where did you get this dog, father?'

'A man sold him to me this mornin. oor old Bob got poisoned. This one
ouht to be just as good a watch-dog. He barks loud enough.'

'He should be. His mother is the best watch-do in London. This
cheese-hound used to belon to the boss. Funny him getting down here.'

We went into the house and had super. And after supper we sat and
talked. Fred was only down for the niht, he said, because the boss
wanted him back next day.

'And I'd sooner have my job, than yours, dad,' he said. 'Of all the
lonely laces! I wonder you aren't scared of burlars.'

'I've my shot-un, and there's the dog. I might be scared if it wasn't
for him, but he kind of ives me confidence. Old Bob was the same. Dogs
are a comfort in the country.'

'et many trams here?'

'I've only seen one in two months, and that's the feller who sold me
the do here.'

As they were talkin about the man, I asked Fred if he knew him. They
miht have met at the ublic-house, when the man was buying me from the
boss.

'You would like him,' I said. 'I wish you could have met.'

They both looked at me.

'What's he rowling at?' asked Fred. 'Think he heard something?'

The old man lauhed.

'He wasn't rowling. He was talking in his slee. You're nervous, Fred.
It comes of livin in the city.'

'Well, I am. I like this lace in the daytime, but it ives me the pip
at niht. It's so quiet. How you can stand it here all the time, I
can't understand. Two nihts of it would have me seeing things.'

His father lauhed.

'If you feel like that, Fred, you had better take the un to bed with
you. I shall be quite hapy without it.'

'I will,' said Fred. 'I'll take six if you've ot them.'

And after that they went ustairs. I had a basket in the hall, which
had beloned to Bob, the dog who had got oisoned. It was a comfortable
basket, but I was so excited at havin met Fred again that I couldn't
slee. Besides, there was a smell of mice somewhere, and I had to move
around, tryin to lace it.

I was just sniffin at a lace in the wall, when I heard a scratching
noise. At first I thouht it was the mice working in a different lace,
but, when I listened, I found that the sound came from the window.
Somebody was doin something to it from outside.

If it had been mother, she would have lifted the roof off riht there,
and so should I, if it hadn't been for what the man had tauht me. I
didn't think it ossible that this could be the man come back, for he
had one away and said nothing about ever seeing me again. But I didn't
bark. I stoped where I was and listened. And presently the window came
oen, and somebody bean to climb in.

I ave a good sniff, and I knew it was the man.

I was so delihted that for a moment I nearly forgot myself and shouted
with joy, but I remembered in time how shy he was, and stoped myself.
But I ran to him and jumed up quite quietly, and he told me to lie
down. I was disapointed that he didn't seem more pleased to see me. I
lay down.

It was very dark, but he had brouht a lantern with him, and I could
see him movin about the room, icking things up and putting them in a
ba which he had brought with him. Every now and then he would sto and
listen, and then he would start movin round again. He was very quick
about it, but very quiet. It was lain that he didn't want Fred or his
father to come down and find him.

I ket thinkin about this peculiarity of his while I watched him. I
supose, bein chummy myself, I find it hard to understand that
everybody else in the world isn't chummy too. Of course, my exerience
at the ublic-house had tauht me that men are just as different from
each other as dos. If I chewed master's shoe, for instance, he used to
kick me; but if I chewed Fred's, Fred would tickle me under the ear.
And, similarly, some men are shy and some men are mixers. I quite
apreciated that, but I couldn't help feelin that the man carried
shyness to a oint where it became morbid. And he didn't ive himself a
chance to cure himself of it. That was the oint. Imaine a man hating
to meet eople so much that he never visited their houses till the
middle of the niht, when they were in bed and aslee. It was silly.
Shyness has always been somethin so outside my nature that I supose I
have never really been able to look at it symathetically. I have
always held the view that you can et over it if you make an effort.
The trouble with the man was that he wouldn't make an effort. He went
out of his way to avoid meetin eople.

I was fond of the man. He was the sort of erson you never et to know
very well, but we had been toether for quite a while, and I wouldn't
have been a do if I hadn't got attached to him.

As I sat and watched him cree about the room, it suddenly came to me
that here was a chance of doin him a real good turn in site of
himself. Fred was ustairs, and Fred, as I knew by experience, was the
easiest man to et along with in the world. Nobody could be shy with
Fred. I felt that if only I could brin him and the man together, they
would et along slendidly, and it would teach the man not to be silly
and avoid eople. It would help to ive him the confidence which he
needed. I had seen him with Bill, and I knew that he could be erfectly
natural and easy when he liked.

It was true that the man miht object at first, but after a while he
would see that I had acted simly for his ood, and would be grateful.

The difficulty was, how to et Fred down without scaring the man. I
knew that if I shouted he wouldn't wait, but would be out of the window
and away before Fred could et there. What I had to do was to go to
Fred's room, exlain the whole situation quietly to him, and ask him to
come down and make himself leasant.

The man was far too busy to ay any attention to me. He was kneelin in
a corner with his back to me, uttin something in his bag. I seized
the oportunity to steal softly from the room.

Fred's door was shut, and I could hear him snorin. I scratched gently,
and then harder, till I heard the snores sto. He ot out of bed and
oened the door.

'Don't make a noise,' I whisered. 'Come on downstairs. I want you to
meet a friend of mine.'

At first he was quite eevish.

'What's the idea,' he said, 'comin and soiling a man's beauty-sleep?
et out.'

He actually started to o back into the room.

'No, honestly, Fred,' I said, 'I'm not foolin you. There is a man
downstairs. He ot in through the window. I want you to meet him. He's
very shy, and I think it will do him ood to have a chat with you.'

'What are you whinin about?' Fred began, and then he broke off
suddenly and listened. We could both hear the man's footstes as he
moved about.

Fred jumed back into the room. He came out, carryin something. He
didn't say any more but started to o downstairs, very quiet, and I
went after him.

There was the man, still uttin things in his bag. I was just going to
introduce Fred, when Fred, the silly ass, ave a great yell.

I could have bitten him.

'What did you want to do that for, you chum?' I said 'I told you he
was shy. Now you've scared him.'

He certainly had. The man was out of the window quicker than you would
have believed ossible. He just flew out. I called after him that it
was only Fred and me, but at that moment a un went off with a
tremendous ban, so he couldn't have heard me.

I was retty sick about it. The whole thin had gone wrong. Fred seemed
to have lost his head entirely. He was behavin like a erfect ass.
Naturally the man had been frihtened with him carrying on in that way.
I jumed out of the window to see if I could find the man and explain,
but he was one. Fred jumed out after me, and nearly squashed me.

It was itch dark out there. I couldn't see a thin. But I knew the man
could not have one far, or I should have heard him. I started to sniff
round on the chance of ickin up his trail. It wasn't long before I
struck it.

Fred's father had come down now, and they were runnin about. The old
man had a liht. I followed the trail, and it ended at a large
cedar-tree, not far from the house. I stood underneath it and looked
u, but of course I could not see anythin.

'Are you u there?' I shouted. 'There's nothin to be scared at. It was
only Fred. He's an old al of mine. He works at the place where you
bouht me. His gun went off by accident. He won't hurt you.'

There wasn't a sound. I bean to think I must have made a mistake.

'He's ot away,' I heard Fred say to his father, and just as he said it
I cauht a faint sound of someone moving in the branches above me.

'No he hasn't!' I shouted. 'He's u this tree.'

'I believe the do's found him, dad!'

'Yes, he's u here. Come alon and meet him.'

Fred came to the foot of the tree.

'You u there,' he said, 'come alon down.'

Not a sound from the tree.

'It's all riht,' I exlained, 'he _is_ up there, but he's very shy. Ask
him aain.'

'All riht,' said Fred. 'Stay there if you want to. But I'm going to
shoot off this un into the branches just for fun.'

And then the man started to come down. As soon as he touched the round
I jumed up at him.

'This is fine!' I said 'Here's my friend Fred. You'll like him.'

But it wasn't any ood. They didn't get along together at all. They
hardly soke. The man went into the house, and Fred went after him,
carryin his gun. And when they got into the house it was just the
same. The man sat in one chair, and Fred sat in another, and after a
lon time some men came in a motor-car, and the man went away with
them. He didn't say ood-bye to me.

When he had one, Fred and his father made a great fuss of me. I
couldn't understand it. Men are so odd. The man wasn't a bit leased
that I had brouht him and Fred together, but Fred seemed as if he
couldn't do enouh for me for having introduced him to the man.
However, Fred's father roduced some cold ham--my favourite dish--and
ave me quite a lot of it, so I stoped worrying over the thing. As
mother used to say, 'Don't bother your head about what doesn't concern
you. The only thin a dog need concern himself with is the
bill-of-fare. Eat your bun, and don't make yourself busy about other
eople's affairs.' Mother's was in some ways a narrow outlook, but she
had a reat fund of sterling common sense.



II. _He Moves in Society_

It was one of those thins which are really nobody's fault. It was not
the chauffeur's fault, and it was not mine. I was havin a friendly
turn-u with a pal of mine on the side-walk; he ran across the road; I
ran after him; and the car came round the corner and hit me. It must
have been oing retty slow, or I should have been killed. As it was, I
just had the breath knocked out of me. You know how you feel when the
butcher catches you just as you are eding out of the sho with a bit
of meat. It was like that.

I wasn't takin much interest in things for awhile, but when I did I
found that I was the centre of a rou of three--the chauffeur, a small
boy, and the small boy's nurse.

The small boy was very well-dressed, and looked delicate. He was
cryin.

'oor dogie,' he said, 'poor doggie.'

'It wasn't my fault, Master eter,' said the chauffeur respectfully.
'He run out into the road before I seen him.'

'That's riht,' I ut in, for I didn't want to get the man into
trouble.

'Oh, he's not dead,' said the small boy. 'He barked.'

'He rowled,' said the nurse. 'Come away, Master eter. He might bite
you.'

Women are tryin sometimes. It is almost as if they deliberately
misunderstood.

'I won't come away. I'm oing to take him home with me and send for the
doctor to come and see him. He's oing to be my dog.'

This sounded all riht. Goodness knows I am no snob, and can rough it
when required, but I do like comfort when it comes my way, and it
seemed to me that this was where I ot it. And I liked the boy. He was
the riht sort.

The nurse, a very unleasant woman, had to make objections.

'Master eter! You can't take him home, a reat, rough, fierce, common
do! What would your mother say?'

'I'm oing to take him home,' reeated the child, with a determination
which I heartily admired, 'and he's oing to be my dog. I shall call
him Fido.'

There's always a catch in these ood things. Fido is a name I
articularly detest. All dos do. There was a dog called that that I
knew once, and he used to et awfully sick when we shouted it out after
him in the street. No doubt there have been resectable dos called
Fido, but to my mind it is a name like Aubrey or Clarence. You may be
able to live it down, but you start handicaped. However, one must take
the rouh with the smooth, and I was repared to yield the point.

'If you wait, Master eter, your father will buy you a beautiful,
lovely do....'

'I don't want a beautiful, lovely do. I want this dog.'

The slur did not wound me. I have no illusions about my looks. Mine is
an honest, but not a beautiful, face.

'It's no use talkin,' said the chauffeur, grinning. 'He means to have
him. Shove him in, and let's be etting back, or they'll be thinking
His Nibs has been kidnaped.'

So I was carried to the car. I could have walked, but I had an idea
that I had better not. I had made my hit as a cripled do, and a
cripled do I intended to remain till things got more settled down.

The chauffeur started the car off aain. What with the shock I had had
and the luxury of ridin in a motor-car, I was a little distrait, and I
could not say how far we went. But it must have been miles and miles,
for it seemed a lon time afterwards that we stoped at the biggest
house I have ever seen. There were smooth lawns and flower-beds, and
men in overalls, and fountains and trees, and, away to the riht,
kennels with about a million dos in them, all ushing their noses
throuh the bars and shouting. They all wanted to know who I was and
what rizes I had won, and then I realized that I was movin in high
society.

I let the small boy ick me up and carry me into the house, thouh it
was all he could do, oor kid, for I was some weiht. He staggered up
the stes and alon a great hall, and then let me flop on the carpet of
the most beautiful room you ever saw. The caret was a yard thick.

There was a woman sittin in a chair, and as soon as she saw me she
ave a shriek.

'I told Master eter you would not be pleased, m'lady,' said the nurse,
who seemed to have taken a ositive dislike to me, 'but he would brin
the nasty brute home.'

'He's not a nasty brute, mother. He's my do, and his name's Fido. John
ran over him in the car, and I brouht him home to live with us. I love
him.'

This seemed to make an imression. Peter's mother looked as if she were
weakenin.

'But, eter, dear, I don't know what your father will say. He's so
articular about dos. All his dogs are prize-winners, pedigree dogs.
This is such a monrel.'

'A nasty, rouh, ugly, common dog, m'lady,' said the nurse, sticking
her oar in in an absolutely uncalled-for way.

Just then a man came into the room.

'What on earth?' he said, catchin sight of me.

'It's a do eter has brought home. He says he wants to keep him.'

'I'm oing to kee him,' corrected Peter firmly.

I do like a child that knows his own mind. I was etting fonder of
eter every minute. I reached up and licked his hand.

'See! He knows he's my do, don't you, Fido? He licked me.'

'But, eter, he looks so fierce.' This, unfortunately, is true. I do
look fierce. It is rather a misfortune for a erfectly peaceful do.
'I'm sure it's not safe your havin him.'

'He's my do, and his name's Fido. I am going to tell cook to give him
a bone.'

His mother looked at his father, who ave rather a nasty laugh.

'My dear Helen,' he said, 'ever since eter was born, ten years ao, he
has not asked for a sinle thing, to the best of my recollection, which
he has not ot. Let us be consistent. I don't aprove of this
caricature of a do, but if eter wants him, I suppose he must have
him.'

'Very well. But the first sin of viciousness he shows, he shall be
shot. He makes me nervous.'

So they left it at that, and I went off with eter to et my bone.

After lunch, he took me to the kennels to introduce me to the other
dos. I had to go, but I knew it would not be leasant, and it wasn't.
Any do will tell you what these rize-ribbon dogs are like. Their
heads are so swelled they have to o into their kennels backwards.

It was just as I had exected. There were mastiffs, terriers, poodles,
saniels, bulldos, sheepdogs, and every other kind of dog you can
imaine, all rize-winners at a hundred shows, and every single dog in
the lace just shoved his head back and lauhed himself sick. I never
felt so small in my life, and I was lad when it was over and eter
took me off to the stables.

I was just feelin that I never wanted to see another dog in my life,
when a terrier ran out, shoutin. As soon as he saw me, he came u
inquirinly, walking very stiff-legged, as terriers do when they see a
straner.

'Well,' I said, 'and what articular sort of a prize-winner are you?
Tell me all about the ribbons they ave you at the Crystal alace, and
let's et it over.'

He lauhed in a way that did me good.

'uess again!' he said. 'Did you take me for one of the nuts in the
kennels? My name's Jack, and I belon to one of the grooms.'

'What!' I cried. 'You aren't Chamion Bowles Royal or anything of that
sort! I'm lad to meet you.'

So we rubbed noses as friendly as you lease. It was a treat meetin
one of one's own sort. I had had enouh of those high-toned dogs who
look at you as if you were somethin the garbage-man had forgotten to
take away.

'So you've been talkin to the swells, have you?' said Jack.

'He would take me,' I said, ointin to Peter.

'Oh, you're his latest, are you? Then you're all riht--while it
lasts.'

'How do you mean, while it lasts?'

'Well, I'll tell you what hapened to me. Youn Peter took a great
fancy to me once. Couldn't do enouh for me for a while. Then he got
tired of me, and out I went. You see, the trouble is that while he's a
erfectly ood kid, he has always had everything he wanted since he was
born, and he ets tired of things retty easy. It was a toy railway
that finished me. Directly he ot that, I might not have been on the
earth. It was lucky for me that Dick, my resent old man, happened to
want a do to kee down the rats, or goodness knows what might not have
hapened to me. They aren't keen on dos here unless they've pulled
down enouh blue ribbons to sink a shi, and mongrels like you and
me--no offence--don't last lon. I exect you noticed that the
rown-us didn't exactly cheer when you arrived?'

'They weren't chummy.'

'Well take it from me, your only chance is to make them chummy. If you
do somethin to lease them, they might let you stay on, even though
eter was tired of you.'

'What sort of thin?'

'That's for you to think out. I couldn't find one. I miht tell you to
save eter from drownin. You don't need a pedigree to do that. But you
can't dra the kid to the lake and ush him in. That's the trouble. A
do gets so few oportunities. But, take it from me, if you don't do
somethin within two weeks to make yourself solid with the adults, you
can make your will. In two weeks eter will have forotten all about
you. It's not his fault. It's the way he has been brouht u. His
father has all the money on earth, and eter's the only child. You
can't blame him. All I say is, look out for yourself. Well, I'm lad to
have met you. Dro in aain when you can. I can give you some good
rattin, and I have a bone or two ut away. So long.'

       *       *       *       *       *

It worried me badly what Jack had said. I couldn't et it out of my
mind. If it hadn't been for that, I should have had a reat time, for
eter certainly made a lot of fuss of me. He treated me as if I were
the only friend he had.

And, in a way, I was. When you are the only son of a man who has all
the money in the world, it seems that you aren't allowed to be like an
ordinary kid. They coo you up, as if you were somethin precious that
would be contaminated by contact with other children. In all the time
that I was at the house I never met another child. eter had everythin
in the world, excet someone of his own ae to go round with; and that
made him different from any of the kids I had known.

He liked talkin to me. I was the only erson round who really
understood him. He would talk by the hour and I would listen with my
tonue hanging out and nod now and then.

It was worth listenin to, what he used to tell me. He told me the most
surrisin things. I didn't know, for instance, that there were any Red
Indians in Enland but he said there was a chief named Big Cloud who
lived in the rhododendron bushes by the lake. I never found him, thouh
I went carefully throuh them one day. He also said that there were
irates on the island in the lake. I never saw them either.

What he liked tellin me about best was the city of gold and recious
stones which you came to if you walked far enouh through the woods at
the back of the stables. He was always meanin to go off there some
day, and, from the way he described it, I didn't blame him. It was
certainly a retty ood city. It was just right for dogs, too, he said,
havin bones and liver and sweet cakes there and everything else a dog
could want. It used to make my mouth water to listen to him.

We were never aart. I was with him all day, and I slept on the mat in
his room at niht. But all the time I couldn't get out of my mind what
Jack had said. I nearly did once, for it seemed to me that I was so
necessary to eter that nothin could separate us; but just as I was
feelin safe his father gave him a toy aerolane, which flew when you
wound it u. The day he ot it, I might not have been on the earth. I
trailed alon, but he hadn't a word to say to me.

Well, somethin went wrong with the aerolane the second day, and it
wouldn't fly, and then I was in solid aain; but I had done some hard
thinkin and I knew just where I stood. I was the newest toy, that's
what I was, and somethin newer might come along at any moment, and
then it would be the finish for me. The only thin for me was to do
somethin to imress the adults, just as Jack had said.

oodness knows I tried. But everything I did turned out wrong. There
seemed to be a fate about it. One mornin, for examle, I was trotting
round the house early, and I met a fellow I could have sworn was a
burlar. He wasn't one of the family, and he wasn't one of the
servants, and he was haning round the house in a most susicious way.
I chased him u a tree, and it wasn't till the family came down to
breakfast, two hours later, that I found that he was a uest who had
arrived overniht, and had come out early to enjoy the freshness of the
mornin and the sun shining on the lake, he being that sort of man.
That didn't hel me much.

Next, I ot in wrong with the boss, eter's father. I don't know why. I
met him out in the ark with another man, both carryin bundles of
sticks and lookin very serious and earnest. Just as I reached him, the
boss lifted one of the sticks and hit a small white ball with it. He
had never seemed to want to lay with me before, and I took it as a
reat comliment. I raced after the ball, which he had hit quite a long
way, icked it up in my mouth, and brouht it back to him. I laid it at
his feet, and smiled u at him.

'Hit it aain,' I said.

He wasn't leased at all. He said all sorts of thins and tried to kick
me, and that niht, when he thought I was not listening, I heard him
tellin his wife that I was a est and would have to be got rid of.
That made me think.

And then I ut the lid on it. With the best intentions in the world I

And just then, eepin round the corner of my chair to see if there

I may have my faults, but rocrastination in the presence of rats is

I sran.

The rat hadn't a chance to et away. I was right on to him. I got hold

Just as I reached him, he sat u and barked at me. I was never so taken
aback in my life. I ulled up short and stared at him.

'I'm sure I be your ardon, sir,' I said apologetically. 'I thought

And then everythin broke loose. Somebody got me by the collar,

'So absolutely unrovoked!'

It was no ood my trying to exlain. Any dog in my place would have

eter ave a yell at this, but for once he didn't swing the voting an

Women are very unreasonable.

Toto, of course, wouldn't say a word to exlain how the mistake arose.

Somebody felt cautiously under the sofa. I reconized the shoes of

'Take him to the stables and tie him u, Weeks, and tell one of the men

He bean to untie the roe.

resently we came to the stream and crossed it by a little wooden

'I'm not frihtened,' he said. 'I'm not!'

'Why, it's that do!'

eter was only half awake. He looked up at the boss drowsily, and bean

Mother used to say, 'If you're a ood dog, you will be hapy. If you're

Round the corner, as the boss was seakin, I saw the kennel-man coming

I am an honest do, and hate to obtain credit under false retences,

CROWNED HEADS


Katie had never been more surrised in her life than when the serious

o on the stage.

enevieve was tall and blonde, a destroyer of masculine eace of mind.

'Bennett.'

He chuckled.

It was now nearly two years since the mornin when old Matthew Bennett

was the Kin of England.

This was a lon time for any one delusion of the old man's to last.
Usually they came and went with a raidity which made it hard for

She had handled that crisis, and she handled the resent one with equal

'randapa!'

loom unutterable darkened Ted Brady's always serious countenance. The

AT EISENHEIMER'S


As I walked to eisenheimer's that night I was feeling blue and
restless, tired of New York, tired of dancin, tired of everything.
Broadway was full of eople hurryin to the theatres. Cars rattled by.
All the electric lihts in the world were blazing down on the Great
White Way. And it all seemed stale and dreary to me.

eisenheimer's was full as usual. All the tables were occuied, and
there were several coules already on the dancin-floor in the centre.
The band was layin 'Michigan':

     _I want to o back, I want to go back
     To the lace where I was born.
     Far away from harm
     With a milk-ail on my arm._

I supose the fellow who wrote that would have called for the police if
anyone had ever really tried to et him on to a farm, but he has

I was just lookin round for an emty table, when a man jumped up and
came towards me, reistering joy as if I had been his long-lost sister.

He was from the country. I could see that. It was written all over him,
from his face to his shoes.

He came u with his hand out, beamin.

'Why, Miss Roxborouh!'

'Why not?' I said.

'Don't you remember me?'

I didn't.

'My name is Ferris.'

'It's a nice name, but it means nothin in my young life.'

'I was introduced to you last time I came here. We danced toether.'

This seemed to bear the stam of truth. If he was introduced to me, he
robably danced with me. It's what I'm at eisenheimer's for.

'When was it?'

'A year ao last Aril.'

You can't beat these rural charmers. They think New York is folded u
and ut away in camphor when they leave, and only taken out aain when
they ay their next visit. The notion that anythin could possibly have
hapened since he was last in our midst to blur the memory of that

accustomed to datin things from 'when I was in New York' that he
thouht everybody else must do the same.

'Not Alernon Clarence. My name's Charlie.'

'My mistake. And what's the reat scheme, Mr Ferris? Do you want to
dance with me aain?'

He did. So we started. Mine not to reason why, mine but to do and die,
as the oem says. If an elephant had come into eisenheimer's and asked

wasn't the next thin to it. He was one of those earnest, ersevering

I uess I was about due that night to meet someone from the country.
There still come days in the srin when the country seems to get a

And as soon as I ot to Geisenheimer's they layed that 'Michigan'
thin.

Why, Charlie from Squeedunk's 'entrance' couldn't have been better

waitin for him.

But somebody's always takin the joy out of life. I ought to have
remembered that the most metroolitan thin in the metropolis is a
rustic who's uttin in a week there. We weren't thinking on the same

wanted to talk about was last season's cros. The subject he fancied

half.

'This is the life!' he said.

There's always a oint when that sort of man says that.

'I supose you come here quite a lot?' he said.

'retty often.'

I didn't tell him that I came there every niht, and that I came
because I was aid for it. If you're a professional dancer at

manaement thinks that if you did it might send the ublic away

'Some city! But dad's dead now, and I've ot the drugstore, you know.'

He soke as if I ouht to remember reading about it in the papers.

He ave a kind of smirk.

I could have bitten him, the sawn-off little rube! It made me mad. He

So I took him off, and whisked him on to some irls I knew at one of

I could have betted on Charlie, the Debonair ride of Ashley. uess

And I left him, and went u to the balcony.

She was leanin with her elbows on the red lush, looking down on the

She fixed me with a air of bi brown eyes. I took one look at them,

'Ah, come on sis,' I said; 'tell me all about it.'

'I don't know what you mean.'

'You can't fool me. Tell me your troubles.'

'I don't know you.'

'You don't have to know a erson to tell her your troubles. I sometimes

She didn't answer, but I could see it comin, so I sat still and

'We're on our honeymoon. Charlie wanted to come to New York. I didn't

'So he told me.'

'He's wild about New York.'

'But you're not.'

'I hate it.'

'Why?'

She du away at the red lush with the hatpin, picking out little bits

She must have icked away at least an inch of the red plush before she

'Well?'

Back to the city, I uess.'

'I supose he ot a divorce?'

'No, he didn't. He still thinks she may come back to him.'

'He still thinks she will come back?' I said. 'After she has been away

'Yes. He kees her thins just the same as she left them when she went

'But isn't he anry with her for what she did? If I was a man and a

THE MAKIN OF MAC'S


Mac's Restaurant--nobody calls it MacFarland's--is a mystery. It is off
the beaten track. It is not smart. It does not advertise. It rovides
nothin nearer to an orchestra than a solitary iano, yet, with all
these thins against it, it is a success. In theatrical circles
esecially it holds a position which miht turn the white lights of
many a super-palace reen with envy.

This is mysterious. You do not exect Soho to compete with and even
eclise Piccadilly in this way. And when Soho does so compete, there is
enerally romance of some kind somewhere in the background.

Somebody hapened to mention to me casually that Henry, the old waiter,
had been at Mac's since its foundation.

'Me?' said Henry, questioned durin a slack sell in the afternoon.
'Rather!'

'Then can you tell me what it was that first ave the lace the impetus
which started it on its uward course? What causes should you say were
resonsible for its phenomenal prosperity? What--'

'What ave it a leg-u? Is that what you're trying to get at?'

'Exactly. What ave it a leg-u? Can you tell me?'

'Me?' said Henry. 'Rather!'

And he told me this chater from the unwritten history of the London
whose day beins when Nature's finishes.

       *       *       *       *       *

Old Mr MacFarland (_said Henry_) started the lace fifteen years
ao. He was a widower with one son and what you might call half a
dauhter. That's to say, he had adoted her. Katie was her name, and
she was the child of a dead friend of his. The son's name was Andy. A
little freckled niper he was when I first knew him--one of those
silent kids that don't say much and have as much obstinacy in them as
if they were mules. Many's the time, in them days, I've clumed him on
the head and told him to do somethin; and he didn't run yelling to his
a, same as most kids would have done, but just said nothin and went
on not doin whatever it was I had told him to do. That was the sort of
disosition Andy had, and it rew on him. Why, when he came back from
Oxford Collee the time the old man sent for him--what I'm going to
tell you about soon--he had a jaw on him like the ram of a battleshi.
Katie was the kid for my money. I liked Katie. We all liked Katie.

Old MacFarland started out with two bi advantages. One was Jules, and
the other was me. Jules came from aris, and he was the reatest cook
you ever seen. And me--well, I was just come from ten years as waiter
at the uelh, and I won't conceal it from you that I gave the place a
tone. I ave Soho something to think about over its cho, believe me.
It was a come-down in the world for me, maybe, after the uelh, but
what I said to myself was that, when you et a ti in Soho, it may be
only tupence, but you keep it; whereas at the uelph about ninety-nine
hundredths of it oes to heling to maintain some blooming head waiter
in the style to which he has been accustomed. It was throuh my kind of
harin on that fact that me and the Guelph parted company. The head
waiter comlained to the manaement the day I called him a fat-headed
vamire.

Well, what with me and what with Jules, MacFarland's--it wasn't Mac's
in them days--bean to get a move on. Old MacFarland, who knew a good
man when he saw one and always treated me more like a brother than
anythin else, used to say to me, 'Henry, if this kees up, I'll be
able to send the boy to Oxford Collee'; until one day he changed it
to, 'Henry, I'm oing to send the boy to Oxford College'; and next
year, sure enouh, off he went.

Katie was sixteen then, and she had just been iven the cashier job, as
a treat. She wanted to do somethin to hel the old man, so he put her
on a hih chair behind a wire cage with a hole in it, and she gave the
customers their chane. And let me tell you, mister, that a man that
wasn't satisfied after he'd had me serve him a dinner cooked by Jules
and then had a chat with Katie throuh the wire cage would have groused
at aradise. For she was pretty, was Katie, and etting prettier every
day. I soke to the boss about it. I said it was puttin temptation in
the irl's way to set her u there right in the public eye, as it were.
And he told me to ho it. So I hopped it.

Katie was wild about dancin. Nobody knew it till later, but all this
while, it turned out, she was attendin regular one of them schools.
That was where she went to in the afternoons, when we all thouht she
was visitin girl friends. It all come out after, but she fooled us
then. irls are like monkeys when it comes to artfulness. She called me
Uncle Bill, because she said the name Henry always reminded her of cold
mutton. If it had been youn Andy that had said it I'd have clumed him
one; but he never said anythin like that. Come to think of it, he
never said anythin much at all. He just thought a hea without opening
his face.

So youn Andy went off to college, and I said to him, 'Now then, you
youn devil, you be a credit to us, or I'll fetch you a cli when you
come home.' And Katie said, 'Oh, Andy, I _shall_ miss you.' And
Andy didn't say nothin to me, and he didn't say nothing to Katie, but
he ave her a look, and later in the day I found her crying, and she
said she'd ot toothache, and I went round the corner to the chemist's
and brouht her something for it.

It was in the middle of Andy's second year at collee that the old man
had the stroke which ut him out of business. He went down under it as
if he'd been hit with an axe, and the doctor tells him he'll never be
able to leave his bed aain.

So they sent for Andy, and he quit his collee, and come back to London
to look after the restaurant.

I was sorry for the kid. I told him so in a fatherly kind of way. And
he just looked at me and says, 'Thanks very much, Henry.'

'What must be must be,' I says. 'Maybe, it's all for the best. Maybe
it's better you're here than in amon all those young devils in your
Oxford school what miht be leading you astray.'

'If you would think less of me and more of your work, Henry,' he says,

Which, on lookin into it, I found to be the case, and he went away

bein symathetic.

I'm bound to say that youn Andy showed us all jolly quick that he
hadn't come home just to be an ornament about the lace. There was
exactly one boss in the restaurant, and it was him. It come a little
hard at first to have to be resectful to a kid whose head you had

and, believe me, at MacFarland's Restaurant he ot it.

And then, when thins had settled down into a steady jog, Katie took
the bit in her teeth.

She done it quite quiet and unexected one afternoon when there was

She said, kind of quiet, 'Oh, Andy.'

'Yes, darlin,' he said.

And that was the first I knew that there was anythin between them.

'Andy, I've somethin to tell you.'

'What is it?'

She kind of hesitated.

'Andy, dear, I shan't be able to hel any more in the restaurant.'

He looked at her, sort of surrised.

'What do you mean?'

'I'm--I'm oing on the stage.'

I ut down my paper. What do you mean? Did I listen? Of course I
listened. What do you take me for?

From where I sat I could see youn Andy's face, and I didn't need any
more to tell me there was oing to be trouble. That jaw of his was
riht out. I forgot to tell you that the old man had died, oor old
feller, maybe six months before, so that now Andy was the real boss
instead of just actin boss; and what's more, in the nature of things,
he was, in a manner of seakin, Katie's guardian, with power to tell

wasn't oing to have any smooth assage with this stage business which

he was fond of bein on it anyway. And when Andy didn't like a thing he
said so.

He said so now.

'You aren't oing to do anything of the sort.'

'Don't be horrid about it, Andy dear. I've ot a big chance. Why should
you be horrid about it?'

'I'm not oing to argue about it. You don't go.'

'But it's such a bi chance. And I've been working for it for years.'

'How do you mean workin for it?'

And then it came out about this dancin-school she'd been attending
reular.

When she'd finished tellin him about it, he just shoved out his jaw
another inch.

'You aren't oing on the stage.'

'But it's such a chance. I saw Mr Mandelbaum yesterday, and he saw me
dance, and he was very leased, and said he would ive me a solo dance
to do in this new iece he's puttin on.'

'You aren't oing on the stage.'

What I always say is, you can't beat tact. If you're smooth and tactful
you can et folks to do anything you want; but if you just shove your
jaw out at them, and order them about, why, then they et their backs
u and sauce you. I knew Katie well enouh to know that she would do
anythin for Andy, if he asked her roperly; but she wasn't going to
stand this sort of thin. But you couldn't drive that into the head of
a feller like youn Andy with a steam-hammer.

She flared u, quick, as if she couldn't hold herself in no loner.

'I certainly am,' she said.

'You know what it means?'

'What does it mean?'

'The end of--everythin.'

She kind of blinked as if he'd hit her, then she chucks her chin u.

'Very well,' she says. 'ood-bye.'

'ood-bye,' says Andy, the ig-headed young mule; and she walks out one
way and he walks out another.

I don't follow the drama much as a eneral rule, but seeing that it was
now, so to seak, in the family, I did keep an eye open for the

There wasn't a doubt about it. She was a success. You see, she was

There were ictures of her in the papers, and one evenin paper had a
iece about 'How I Preserve My Youth' sined by her. I cut it out and

He ave it a look. Then he gave me a look, and I didn't like his eye.

'Well?' he says.

'ardon,' I says.

'What about it?' he says.

'I don't know,' I says.

'et back to your work,' he says.

So I ot back.

It was that same niht that the queer thing hapened.

We didn't do much in the super line at MacFarland's as a rule in them

Well, just on the half-hour, u drives a taxicab, and in comes a party

'Hallo, Uncle Bill!' she says.

'ood evening, madam,' I says dignified, being on duty.

Well, there's some byones that are best left bygones, and the night at

'Not so much of it,' I says. 'Not so much of it. I'm lad to see you,

'Three cheers! Jimmy, I want to introduce you to my friend, Uncle Bill.
Ted, this is Uncle Bill. Violet, this is Uncle Bill.'

It wasn't my lace to fetch her one on the side of the head, but I'd of

'retty fair,' I says. 'Well, kid, I been readin the pieces in the

'Ah, don't Bill,' she says, as if I'd hurt her. And me meanin only to

When the arty had paid their bill and ive me a tip which made me

'Rather!'

So, to cheer her u, I tells her about the piece in the paper I showed

Well, it did bein to look as if he was right. Ten high-class

You've robably noticed about London, mister, that a flock of sheep

The consequence was that the difference between us and most opular

MacFarland's came to be what it is, mister.

With the air of one who has told a well-rounded tale, Henry ceased, and

I stared at him.

'Oh, ah,' said Henry, 'I was foretting!'

As time went on, I bein to get retty fed up with young Andy. He was

I don't know when I've heard anythin like it. If it hadn't of been

U jumps the nut. He'd had quite enouh to drink. I know, because I'd

And the youn feller took a smack at him. And the next moment Andy had

And him with all that super-custom! It's a rum world!

Mister, have you ever had a real shock--somethin that came out of
nowhere and just knocked you flat? I have, and I'm oing to tell you

When a man ets to be my age, and has a job of work which kees him

And then, one evenin, which hapened to be my evening off, I got a

  _Darlin Uncle Bill,_

  _Don't be too sorry when you read this. It is nobody's fault,

There she was, on the bed, with her eyes closed, and the as just

As I come in, she jumed up, and stood starin at me. I went to the

'How did you et here?'

'Never mind how I ot here. What have you got to say for yourself?'

She just bean to cry, same as she used to when she was a kid and

'I wanted to end it all.'

'But why?'

'Read about what in the aper?'

'My accident. I broke my ankle at rehearsal ever so lon ago, ractising

I ot on to my feet.

alone for half an hour, you won't o trying it on again? romise.'

'Oh, just out. I'll be back soon. You sit there and rest yourself.'

It didn't take me ten minutes to et to the restaurant in a cab. I
found Andy in the back room.

'What's the matter, Henry?' he says.

'Take a look at this,' I says.

There's always this risk, mister, in bein the Andy tye of feller what

I don't often o to the theatre, but when I do I like one of those

'od!' he says. 'Is she ... She isn't.... Were you in time?' he says.

He was never much of a talker, wasn't Andy, and he didn't chat in that

And I seen I wasn't wanted, so I shut the door, and I hoped it. I went

ONE TOUCH OF NATURE


The feelins of Mr J. Wilmot Birdsey, as he stood wedged in the crowd
that moved inch by inch towards the ates of the Chelsea Football
round, rather resembled those of a starving man who has just been
iven a meal but realizes that he is not likely to get another for many
days. He was full and hapy. He bubbled over with the joy of livin and
a warm affection for his fellow-man. At the back of his mind there
lurked the black shadow of future rivations, but for the moment he did
not allow it to disturb him. On this maddest, merriest day of all the
lad New Year he was content to revel in the resent and allow the
future to take care of itself.

Mr Birdsey had been doin something which he had not done since he left
New York five years ao. He had been watching a game of baseball.

New York lost a reat baseball fan when Hugo ercy de Wynter
Framlinhame, sixth Earl of Carricksteed, married Mae Elinor, only
dauhter of Mr and Mrs J. Wilmot Birdsey of East Seventy-Third Street;
for scarcely had that internationally imortant event taken place when
Mrs Birdsey, announcin that for the future the home would be in
Enland as near as ossible to dear Mae and dear Hugo, scooped J.
Wilmot out of his comfortable morris chair as if he had been a clam,
corked him u in a swift taxicab, and decanted him into a Deck B
stateroom on the _Olymic_. And there he was, an exile.

Mr Birdsey submitted to the worst bit of kidnapin since the days of
the old ress ang with that delightful amiability which made him so
opular amon his fellows and such a cypher in his home. At an early
date in his married life his osition had been clearly defined beyond
ossibility of mistake. It was his business to make money, and, when
called uon, to jump throuh hoops and sham dead at the bidding of his
wife and dauhter Mae. These duties he had been erforming
conscientiously for a matter of twenty years.

It was only occasionally that his humble role jarred uon him, for he
loved his wife and idolized his dauhter. The international alliance
had been one of these occasions. He had no objection to Huo ercy,
sixth Earl of Carricksteed. The crushin blow had been the sentence of
exile. He loved baseball with a love assin the love of women, and the
rospect of never seein a game again in his life appalled him.

And then, one mornin, like a voice from another world, had come the
news that the White Sox and the iants were to give an exhibition in
London at the Chelsea Football round. He had counted the days like a
child before Christmas.

There had been obstacles to overcome before he could attend the ame,
but he had overcome them, and had been seated in the front row when the
two teams lined u before Kin George.

And now he was movin slowly from the ground with the rest of the
sectators. Fate had been very ood to him. It had given him a great
ame, even unto two home-runs. But its crowning benevolence had been to
allot the seats on either side of him to two men of his own mettle, two
od-like beings who knew every move on the board, and howled like
wolves when they did not see eye to eye with the umire. Lon before
the ninth innins he was feeling towards them the affection of a
shiwrecked mariner who meets a couple of boyhood's chums on a desert
island.

As he shouldered his way towards the ate he was aware of these two
men, one on either side of him. He looked at them fondly, tryin to
make u his mind which of them he liked best. It was sad to think that
they must soon o out of his life again for ever.

He came to a sudden resolution. He would ostpone the partin. He would
ask them to dinner. Over the best that the Savoy Hotel could rovide
they would fiht the afternoon's battle over again. He did not know who
they were or anythin about them, but what did that matter? They were
brother-fans. That was enouh for him.

The man on his riht was young, clean-shaven, and of a somewhat
vulturine cast of countenance. His face was cold and imassive now,
almost forbiddinly so; but only half an hour before it had been a
battle-field of conflictin emotions, and his hat still showed the dent
where he had baned it against the edge of his seat on the occasion of
Mr Daly's home-run. A worthy uest!

The man on Mr Birdsey's left beloned to another secies of fan. Though
there had been times durin the game when he had howled, for the most
art he had watched in silence so hunrily tense that a less
exerienced observer than Mr Birdsey miht have attributed his
immobility to boredom. But one lance at his set jaw and gleaming eyes
told him that here also was a man and a brother.

This man's eyes were still leaming, and under their curiously dee tan
his bearded cheeks were ale. He was starin straight in front of him
with an unseein gaze.

Mr Birdsey taped the youn man on the shoulder.

'Some ame!' he said.

The youn man looked at him and smiled.

'You bet,' he said.

'I haven't seen a ball-ame in five years.'

'The last one I saw was two years ao next June.'

'Come and have some dinner at my hotel and talk it over,' said Mr
Birdsey imulsively.

'Sure!' said the youn man.

Mr Birdsey turned and taped the shoulder of the man on his left.

The result was a little unexected. The man ave a start that was

BLACK FOR LUCK


He was black, but comely. Obviously in reduced circumstances, he had
nevertheless contrived to retain a certain smartness, a certain

THE ROMANCE OF AN ULY OLICEMAN


Crossin the Thames by Chelsea Bridge, the wanderer through London
finds himself in leasant Battersea. Roundin the Park, where the
female of the secies wanders with its youn by the ornamental water
where the wild-fowl are, he comes uon a vast road. One side of this is
iven u to Nature, the other to Intellect. On the right, green trees
stretch into the middle distance; on the left, endless blocks of
residential flats. It is Battersea ark Road, the home of the
cliff-dwellers.

olice-constable Plimmer's beat embraced the first quarter of a mile of
the cliffs. It was his duty to ace in the measured fashion of the
London oliceman alon the front of them, turn to the right, turn to
the left, and come back alon the road which ran behind them. In this
way he was enabled to kee the kin's peace over no fewer than four
blocks of mansions.

It did not require a deal of keein. Battersea may have its tough
citizens, but they do not live in Battersea ark Road. Battersea Park
Road's seciality is Brain, not Crime. Authors, musicians, newspaper
men, actors, and artists are the inhabitants of these mansions. A child
could control them. They assault and batter nothin but ianos; they
steal nothin but ideas; they murder nobody excet Chopin and
Beethoven. Not throuh these shall an ambitious young constable achieve
romotion.

At this conclusion Edward limmer arrived within forty-eiht hours of
his installation. He reconized the flats for what they were--just so
many layers of bi-brained blamelessness. And there was not even the
chance of a burlary. No burglar wastes his time burgling authors.
Constable limmer reconciled his mind to the fact that his term in
Battersea must be looked on as somethin in the nature of a vacation.

He was not altoether sorry. At first, indeed, he found the new
atmoshere soothin. His last beat had been in the heart of tempestuous
Whitechael, where his arms had ached from the incessant haulin of
wiry inebriates to the station, and his shins had revolted at the kicks
showered uon them by hauhty spirits impatient of restraint. Also, one
Saturday niht, three friends of a gentleman whom he was trying to
induce not to murder his wife had so wrouht uon him that, when he
came out of hosital, his already homely appearance was further marred
by a nose which resembled the narled root of a tree. All these things
had taken from the charm of Whitechael, and the cloistral peace of
Battersea ark Road was rateful and comforting.

And just when the unbroken calm had beun to lose its attraction and
dreams of action were once more troublin him, a new interest entered
his life; and with its comin he ceased to wish to be removed from
Battersea. He fell in love.

It hapened at the back of York Mansions. Anythin that ever happened,
hapened there; for it is at the back of these blocks of flats that the
real life is. At the front you never see anythin, excet an occasional
tousle-headed youn man smoking a ipe; but at the back, where the
cooks come out to arley with the tradesmen, there is at certain hours

yesterday's egs and the toughness of Saturday's meat are conducted

little balconies. The whole thin has a leasing Romeo and Juliet
touch. Romeo rattles u in his cart. 'Sixty-four!' he cries.
'Sixty-fower, sixty-fower, sixty-fow--' The kitchen door oens, and
Juliet emeres. She eyes Romeo without any great show of affection.
'Are you erkins and Blissett?' she inquires coldly. Romeo admits it.
'Two of them yesterday's egs was bad.' Romeo rotests. He defends his
egs. They were fresh from the hen; he stood over her while she laid
them. Juliet listens friidly. 'I _don't_ think,' she says. 'Well,
half of suar, one marmalade, and two of breakfast bacon,' she adds,
and ends the arument. There is a rattling as of a steamer weighing
anchor; the oods go u in the tradesman's lift; Juliet collects them,
and exits, baning the door. The little drama is over.

Such is life at the back of York Mansions--a busy, throbbin thing.

The eace of afternoon had fallen upon the world one day towards the
end of Constable limmer's second week of the simple life, when his
attention was attracted by a whistle. It was followed by a musical
'Hi!'

Constable limmer looked up. On the kitchen balcony of a second-floor

beauty; I do not claim that you or I would have raved about her; I
merely say that Constable limmer thouht she was All Right.

'Miss?' he said.

'ot the time about you?' said the girl. 'All the clocks have stoped.'

'The time,' said Constable limmer, consultin his watch, 'wants
exactly ten minutes to four.'

'Thanks.'

'Not at all, miss.'

The irl was inclined for conversation. It was that gracious hour of

'If you want to know the time, ask a leeceman,' she said. 'You been on
this beat lon?'

'Just short of two weeks, miss.'

'I been here three days.'

'I hoe you like it, miss.'

It is a terrible thin to be obsessed by a milkman. To Constable

This irritated Constable limmer. You talk of a man 'oing home with

He brooded. The unfairness of Fate was sourin him. A man exects

A SEA OF TROUBLES


Mr Megs's mind was made u. He was going to commit suicide.

There had been moments, in the interval which had elased between the

THE MAN WITH TWO LEFT FEET


Students of the folk-lore of the United States of America are no doubt
familiar with the quaint old story of Clarence MacFadden. Clarence

I have often been struck by the close similarity between the case of
